Mark Rabey I try to make the web a better place by writing the best possible code I can, I trying to share my knowledge with others. http://markrabey.com/ Thu, 30 Oct 2014 09:13:43 -0400 Thu, 30 Oct 2014 09:13:43 -0400 Jekyll v2.3.0 HTML Basics <p>I recently began working with someone who wants to learn how to build his website, rather than simply have me build it for him. As a web developer I’d obviously appreciate the longer term relationship of building and maintaining a site. Let’s be honest, that would likely make me more money than teaching people how to do it themselves. However, this process will actually be really fun. For me, the best part about web development is <em>not</em> updating and maintaining a site. It’s about taking an idea and seeing it come to life. To me <em>that</em> is exciting. I’m really looking forward to this! Unlike most of my posts, this one is geared towards <em>beginners</em>. I am doing my best to assume no prior knowledge of HTML, CSS, or JavaScript. I <em>will</em> however assume you have basic knowledge of how to use a computer. If you feel I’ve failed in this regard, or you have any questions, feel free to leave a comment below, or email me: <a href="&#109;&#097;&#105;&#108;&#116;&#111;:&#104;&#101;&#108;&#108;&#111;&#064;&#109;&#097;&#114;&#107;&#114;&#097;&#098;&#101;&#121;&#046;&#099;&#111;&#109;">&#104;&#101;&#108;&#108;&#111;&#064;&#109;&#097;&#114;&#107;&#114;&#097;&#098;&#101;&#121;&#046;&#099;&#111;&#109;</a>.</p> <h2 id="getting-started">Getting Started</h2> <p>For the purposes of this post, we’re not looking at builders, generators, frameworks, or even styling the page. We’re looking at the absolute <em>basics</em> of building a web page by gaining an <em>understanding</em> of how HTML works within the browser. To do this, we can use our favourite text editor. Personally, I use <a href="http://www.sublimetext.com/3">Sublime Text 3</a>, but you can use whatever you would like. If you’re using Windows, first my condolences, but at least it includes Notepad which is a good enough text editor for what we’re doing here. If you’re using a Mac, you can use TextEdit if you’d rather not get Sublime (although I don’t know why you wouldn’t). Through these examples, we’ll be building a site for a hypothetical computer services company.</p> <h2 id="what-is-html">What is HTML?</h2> <p>HTML stands for <strong>H</strong>yper <strong>T</strong>ext <strong>M</strong>arkup <strong>L</strong>anguage. It is <em>not</em> a programming language, because it does not contain logic that is processed. An HTML file is basically just a text files that is “marked up” to tell web browsers how they should display content.</p> <h2 id="what-does-the-browser-do">What does the browser do?</h2> <p>There are many different web browsers, the most popular being <a href="https://encrypted.google.com/chrome/browser/index.html">Chrome</a>, <a href="https://www.mozilla.org/en-US/firefox/new/">FireFox</a>, and <a href="http://www.newser.com/story/186056/homeland-security-avoid-internet-explorer.html">Internet Explorer</a>. When a browser enters a web page, it receives data from the server in the form of HTML (usually, but that’s a whole other topic). The browser then reads that HTML and displays the page based on that processed HTML. Sometimes browsers, especially Internet Explorer, don’t display the content in the way the author expected, so it’s important to follow HTML guidelines, as well as test in the most popular browsers.</p> <h2 id="html-tags">HTML Tags</h2> <p>Tags are the “markup” part of HTML. Typically we add an opening tag and a closing tag around portions of text in order to give them some sort of context. A basic HTML structure might look something like this:</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="cp">&lt;!DOCTYPE html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;body&gt;</span> This is our web page <span class="nt">&lt;/body&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/html&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <p>In your text editor, put this code, and save the file as <code>index.html</code>. The first line, <code>&lt;!DOCTYPE html&gt;</code> is the <strong>document type declaration</strong>. This tells the browser what sort of document you are try to produce. In this case, it’s <em>HTML5</em>. This can be quite important, as if you don’t include it, the browser will have to guess, and that could cause very unexpected results. Getting back to tags in general, we see <code>&lt;html&gt;</code>. This is the <strong>opening tag</strong> that tells the browser everything between here and the closing tag <code>&lt;/html&gt;</code> is, in fact, HTML. The stuff between <code>&lt;body&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;/body&gt;</code> is the main content of the page. This is what will appear in the browser window.</p> <h2 id="closing-tags">Closing Tags</h2> <p>In our sample, using <code>&lt;/body&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;/html&gt;</code> closes their respective <em>elements</em>. Note that not all tags have a corresponding closing tag. Tags that don’t wrap content don’t usually need a closing tag, and will actually close themselves. A line-break, for example, is represented in HTML as <code>&lt;br&gt;</code>. Because there is no content associated with this tag, it doesn’t have a closing counterpart. The rule of thumb is, “if it wraps content, it needs a closing tag”. Strictly speaking, closing tags are <em>not</em> always required, but it’s good practice, and makes code easier to read.</p> <p>You might come across “self-closing”, where the <code>br</code> tag might appear as <code>&lt;br /&gt;</code> rather than simply <code>&lt;br&gt;</code>. This is a result of older versions of HTML that were based on another language called <strong>XML</strong>. HTML5 no longer requires this format but will simply ignore them if they are there. Some developers have a preference one way or the other, often just out of habit. Personally, I prefer the simpler format.</p> <p>At this point, if you save the above code and open that file with a web browser, you should see something like <a href="/demos/basic-html-1.html" target="_blank">this</a>.</p> <h2 id="attributes">Attributes</h2> <p>Tags can also have <em>attributes</em>, which are extra piece of information the browser can use to render the element. Attributes appear inside the opening tag and their values are surrounded by quotes (usually doubles). They appear something like: <code>&lt;tag arrtibute="value"&gt;content&lt;/tag&gt;</code>. We’ll see more of these in future posts.</p> <h2 id="elements">Elements</h2> <p>Tags essentially do little more than mark the beginning and ending of an element. For example, <code>&lt;body&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;/body</code> are <em>tags</em>, and everything between these, and <em>including them</em>, is what makes up the body <strong><em>element</em></strong>. Another example is <code>&lt;title&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;/title&gt;</code> are tags, and <code>&lt;title&gt;Page Title&lt;/title&gt;</code> is the title <strong><em>element</em></strong>.</p> <h2 id="page-titles">Page Titles</h2> <p>To add a title to your page that will appear at the top of your browser window, change your code to look like this:</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="cp">&lt;!DOCTYPE html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;head&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;title&gt;</span>Computer Service Company<span class="nt">&lt;/title&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/head&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;body&gt;</span> This is our web page <span class="nt">&lt;/body&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/html&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <p>We have added 2 new elements, <code>head</code> and <code>title</code>. The first starts with <code>&lt;head&gt;</code> and ends with <code>&lt;/head&gt;</code>. The second is <em>inside</em> of the <code>head</code> element and starts with <code>&lt;title&gt;</code> and ends with <code>&lt;/title&gt;</code>. The <code>head</code> element is located above the <code>body</code> element and contains information <em>about</em> the page. This content is not displayed in the browser window. There are a number of elements that can appear inside the <code>head</code>, and we’ll get to those eventually, but for now we’ll just include the most important, <code>title</code> element.</p> <p>Now, after saving your code, and refreshing the page, things should look like <a href="/demos/basic-html-2.html" target="_blank">this</a>.</p> <h2 id="paragraphs">Paragraphs</h2> <p>Hopefully you have general idea what I’m talking about here. It’s hard without seeing it in action. If you’re totally confused, reread the above sections, if you’re still stuck, send me an email: <a href="&#109;&#097;&#105;&#108;&#116;&#111;:&#104;&#101;&#108;&#108;&#111;&#064;&#109;&#097;&#114;&#107;&#114;&#097;&#098;&#101;&#121;&#046;&#099;&#111;&#109;">&#104;&#101;&#108;&#108;&#111;&#064;&#109;&#097;&#114;&#107;&#114;&#097;&#098;&#101;&#121;&#046;&#099;&#111;&#109;</a>. If we’re clear so far, we’re ready to start adding some content to the page.</p> <p>Let’s add a line of text.</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="cp">&lt;!DOCTYPE html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;head&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;title&gt;</span>Computer Service Company<span class="nt">&lt;/title&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/head&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;body&gt;</span> This is our web page. We offer virus scan and removal, full system clean, upgrades, diagnosis, and all general repairs. <span class="nt">&lt;/body&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/html&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <p>View this code in your browser likely has some unexpected results. You might expect the text to appear on 2 lines, just the way you typed it, however, instead you will see something like <a href="/demos/basic-html-3.html" target="_blank">this</a>.</p> <p>This is because the browser doesn’t pay any attention to what line your code is on. It doesn’t care about extra spaces either. If you want to have your code on 2 different lines, or in 2 distinct blocks of text, you need to explicitly <em>tell</em> the browser that. Remember, HTML is about <em>meaning</em> not actual presentation.</p> <p>To change this to what we actually mean, we will add <code>p</code> tags to our text. This is used to define <strong>paragraphs</strong> in HTML.</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="cp">&lt;!DOCTYPE html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;head&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;title&gt;</span>Computer Service Company<span class="nt">&lt;/title&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/head&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;body&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;p&gt;</span>This is our web page.<span class="nt">&lt;/p&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;p&gt;</span>We offer virus scan and removal, full system clean, upgrades, diagnosis, and all general repairs.<span class="nt">&lt;/p&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/body&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/html&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <p>Now, <a href="/demos/basic-html-4.html" target="_blank">this</a> should be more what we want. Remember that fonts, colours, positioning, etc. will all be handled <em>later</em> with CSS.</p> <p>Moving on…</p> <h2 id="headings">Headings</h2> <p>The <code>p</code> tag is just he beginning of text formating. A key piece to keeping a document organised is headings. These are also valuable for Search Engine Optimisation (SEO).</p> <p>The heading tags are <code>h1</code>,<code>h2</code>,<code>h3</code>,<code>h4</code>,<code>h5</code>, and, you guessed it, <code>h6</code>. The <code>h1</code> tag is the most important, while <code>h6</code> is low man on the totem pole. For SEO reasons, we should only have one <code>h1</code> element on a page. This should be the <em>most</em> important item in the context of that page.</p> <p>For example, on my home page, my <code>h1</code> is: <code>&lt;h1 class="page-head__text"&gt;Mark Rabey&lt;br&gt;Full-Stack Web Developer&lt;/h1&gt;</code>. the <code>class</code> attribute is used for styling, and not need for our purposes here, and the <code>&lt;br&gt;</code> is for display purposes. On this page, that is the content that is most important. It is what the page is about. It’s about me, and the fact that I am a <a href="/blog/2014/08/15/the-full-stack-web-developer">full-stack web developer</a>. On my blog posts, however, this is no longer what the page is about. So, my <code>h1</code> contains the title of that post. All my other heading tags depend on how they might relate to my <code>h1</code>. You can have many <code>h2</code> - <code>h6</code> tags without affecting your SEO at all, but again, only <em>one</em> <code>h1</code>. The others should be used in order of intended relevance in relation to the <code>h1</code>.</p> <p>Let’s add some headings to our page.</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="cp">&lt;!DOCTYPE html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;head&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;title&gt;</span>Computer Service Company<span class="nt">&lt;/title&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/head&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;body&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;h1&gt;</span>Computer Service Company<span class="nt">&lt;/h1&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;h2&gt;</span>What this is<span class="nt">&lt;/h2&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;p&gt;</span>This is our web page.<span class="nt">&lt;/p&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;h2&gt;</span>What we do<span class="nt">&lt;/h2&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;p&gt;</span>We offer virus scan and removal, full system clean, upgrades, diagnosis, and all general repairs.<span class="nt">&lt;/p&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/body&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/html&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <p>Notice when we look at <a href="/demos/basic-html-5.html" target="_blank">our page</a> now, headings are rendered quite differently. Although we’re not using HTML to style our document, the browser still renders the different headings with different font sizes and weights. The browsers do this so that if something somehow went horribly wrong with your CSS, your users will still have a somewhat valuable experience reading you page.</p> <p>Continuing on, there are a few directions I can take. We could look into <strong>all</strong> the tags, but who has time for that? We could look at a few more common tags, but we might not need them right away. So, we’re going to continue just building our site, and as we hit tags we haven’t used yet, I’ll explain them as needed. Sound good? Let’s go!</p> <h2 id="navigation">Navigation</h2> <p>Adding navigation to your page is obviously key to allowing your users to move around your site easily. We also want our navigation to allow screen readers (for the visually impaired) and web crawlers (for search engines like Google) to also be able to move around your site easily. In our case, we’re going to add our navigation right at the top of the page. Eventually, this will be a nice looking navigation bar, but first we need to just get it on the page. Again, the styling is all coming later. Let’s add to our <code>body</code> element.</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="cp">&lt;!DOCTYPE html&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;html&gt;</span> ... <span class="nt">&lt;body&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;nav&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;ul&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;li&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;a</span> <span class="na">href=</span><span class="s">&quot;services.html&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span> Services <span class="nt">&lt;/a&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/li&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;li&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;a</span> <span class="na">href=</span><span class="s">&quot;products.html&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span> Products <span class="nt">&lt;/a&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/li&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;li&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;a</span> <span class="na">href=</span><span class="s">&quot;about.html&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span> About Us <span class="nt">&lt;/a&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/li&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;li&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;a</span> <span class="na">href=</span><span class="s">&quot;contact.html&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span> Contact Us <span class="nt">&lt;/a&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/li&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/ul&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/nav&gt;</span> ... <span class="nt">&lt;/body&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/html&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <p>We can see what we’ve built <a href="/demos/basic-html-6.html" target="_blank">here</a>. First we added a <code>nav</code> element. This is used to tell the browser that the contents are used for <em>nav</em>igation purposes. Inside of that, we have a <code>ul</code> element. This is an <em>u</em>nordered <em>l</em>ist. Had I used an <code>ol</code> (ordered list) we would see the numbers 1-4 instead of the bullets. I chose to use an unordered list, because although I want them to display in a particular order, the items in the list have no less <em>meaning</em> if they are in any other order. Inside of our <code>ul</code> we have several <code>li</code> elements. These are our <em>l</em>ist <em>i</em>tems. Finally, nested inside our <code>li</code> elements, we see <code>a</code> tags. These are <em>a</em>nchors and represent links to other pages or content. We currently are using only one attribute with our anchor tags. This is the <code>href</code> attribute. This is the <em>h</em>yperlink <em>ref</em>erence, and tell the browser the <strong>destination</strong> of the link. Again, we’ll see here that the browser has added some default styling to our links in order to give them some context until we are able to style them ourselves.</p> <hr /> <p>This is the very <em>basic</em> beginnings of an HTML document, but hopefully this gives you at least some understanding of how HTML is structured. I’ll soon be posting on how one might go about styling this page to have it actually looks like something useful. First we’ll look at getting started with CSS using <a href="http://getbootstrap.com">Bootstrap</a>, then using our own CSS. During this process we’ll also look at expanding on the content of the site. Finally, we’ll look at hosting that content somewhere.</p> <p>For a little more on HTML tags, check out my post on <a href="/blog/2014/05/14/semantic-html">Semantic HTML</a>. Consider <a href="/blog/2014/04/25/title-tags">this post</a> for tips on creating title tags, and <a href="/blog/2014/07/02/meta-descriptions">this one</a> for meta descriptions.</p> Wed, 22 Oct 2014 00:00:00 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/10/22/html-basics http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/10/22/html-basics html, semantics, layout, basics, tutorial Vertical Gap Between Divs <p>Recently, I discovered a very simple solution to what I believe is a pretty major problem. I first encountered the issue when first building <a href="/blog/2014/04/07/making-the-grid-responsive/">my own grid</a>, and I kind of “hacked” around it. I’ve been working on rewriting that grid (coming soon), and decided to find a better solution.</p> <h2 id="the-symptom">The Symptom</h2> <p>In a word: “whitespace”. Simplest example I can think of, is 2 <code>&lt;div&gt;</code>s with CSS that includes <code>display: inline-block;</code> and <code>width: 50%;</code> <em>should</em> be placed side by side. <strong>BUT THEY ARE NOT</strong></p> <p>Take a look:</p> <p data-height="260" data-theme-id="8481" data-slug-hash="JrEpL" data-default-tab="result" data-user="MarkRabey" class="codepen">See the Pen <a href="http://codepen.io/MarkRabey/pen/JrEpL/">Grid System Demo</a> by Mark Rabey (<a href="http://codepen.io/MarkRabey">@MarkRabey</a>) on <a href="http://codepen.io">CodePen</a>.</p> <script async="" src="//codepen.io/assets/embed/ei.js"></script> <h2 id="the-solutionsmaybe">The Solution(s)…maybe</h2> <p>There are many ways I could have solved this. Simply adding <code>float: left;</code> to the <code>.grid__item</code> class and that would work, but it’s not really accurate. In the context of this grid, the elements are <em>not</em> floating. They are placed beside each other. This is essentially what I ended up doing in that first grid, but it lead to issues later having to clear that float.</p> <p>Of course making them slightly less than 50% width makes them appear on the same line, but that’s not quite right. I can play with margins, but that doesn’t really make sense. Again, trying to be somewhat semantic in my thinking, each <code>.grid__item</code> is independent, so adjusting the margin and padding to accommodate others isn’t really accurate either. </p> <p>There are a few other CSS hacks, but these aren’t likely to work in every browser, and may not work forever (I might post about these soon too). I don’t really want to rely on CSS to fix the issue at all, because it’s not really a CSS problem.</p> <h2 id="the-problem">The Problem</h2> <p>The actual problem lies in the HTML itself. Or at least in how some browsers render the HTML. That whitespace between the two <code>.grid__item</code>s is actually rendered as a small gap. Currently in my browser this gap is 5 pixels. I’m honestly not sure if this changes or why it’s there, but I want it gone!</p> <h2 id="the-actual-solution-mine-at-least">The <em>Actual</em> Solution (mine at least)</h2> <p>Remove the whitespace! I don’t want to just remove that gap. I still need to be able to read the code, and that won’t go well if I remove the gap entirely. I came up with 2 solutions. In both cases I am <em>leaving the CSS alone</em>! I’m not sure which on I prefer yet. In one, I add an empty comment (<code>&lt;!-- --&gt;</code>) between the <code>&lt;div&gt;</code>s. The other one is to not close one <code>&lt;div&gt;</code> until the next. Here’s what that looks like:</p> <div data-height="268" data-theme-id="8481" data-slug-hash="Bqdxs" data-default-tab="html" data-user="MarkRabey" class="codepen"><pre><code>&lt;div class=&quot;grid&quot;&gt; &lt;div class=&quot;grid__item&quot;&gt; &lt;p class=&quot;demo-content&quot;&gt; Grid Item 1 &lt;/p&gt; &lt;/div&gt;&lt;!-- --&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;grid__item&quot;&gt; &lt;p class=&quot;demo-content&quot;&gt; Grid Item 2 &lt;/p&gt; &lt;/div&gt;&lt;!-- --&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;grid__item&quot;&gt; &lt;p class=&quot;demo-content&quot;&gt; Grid Item 3 &lt;/p&gt; &lt;/div &gt;&lt;div class=&quot;grid__item&quot;&gt; &lt;p class=&quot;demo-content&quot;&gt; Grid Item 4 &lt;/p&gt; &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/div&gt;</code></pre> <p>See the Pen <a href="http://codepen.io/MarkRabey/pen/Bqdxs/">Grid System Demo</a> by Mark Rabey (<a href="http://codepen.io/MarkRabey">@MarkRabey</a>) on <a href="http://codepen.io">CodePen</a>.</p> </div> <script async="" src="//codepen.io/assets/embed/ei.js"></script> <p>I’m thinking I prefer the latter method. It seems more readable, but I’ll likely go back and forth for few weeks before deciding for sure. Thoughts and comments appreciated.</p> Fri, 19 Sep 2014 00:00:00 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/09/19/vertical-gap-between-divs http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/09/19/vertical-gap-between-divs css, html, grid layout Keeping Up To Date <p>As a developer, I often hear that developers need to keep up to date with new technologies. They need to be continuously learning new things. For a lot of people, this seems to raise a few questions, the most common of which seem to be: <strong>why</strong>, <strong>when</strong>, and <strong>how</strong>?</p> <p>These are all very valid questions, and I honestly haven’t really thought about deep seeded answers to them, which is actually why I’m writing this post. Although I personally do try to learn new things, and I tell my co-workers that they should too, I’ve never really thought about why, how, or when this should be done.</p> <h2 id="why-upgrade">Why Upgrade?</h2> <p>There are several reasons to upgrade your skills. First, let’s consider your job. If you’re working for an agency, it makes sense that you keep up to date in order to offer your clients cutting edge technologies. What if you develop internal, or proprietary systems? Your users still deserve the best experience you can give them. And your managers deserve to have you work as efficiently and effectively as possible. So, why not try something like Grunt for automating tasks, or Vagrant to maintain development environments? Sure, there is likely still a need for “the way we’ve always done it”, but why not push that? Why not consider there might be (and likely is) a better way?</p> <p>If not for your work, do it for yourself. Maybe you aren’t in a position to implement new technologies or ideas in the workplace, sometimes it’s just too hard tom institute that kind of change. But that doesn’t mean there aren’t benefits to learning new things. First of all, if you’re stuck doing the same old thing day in and day out, you can get <em>really</em> bored. You start losing your creativity. You will actually find it harder to solve problems when the <em>do</em> arise, because you’ve spent so much time thinking inside such a narrow box. If you ever want to move beyond that position of doing the same old thing, you’ll need these new skills at any place outside of where you currently work.</p> <p>Typically new technologies emerge for a reason. They don’t just exist for the sake of existing. They are created to solve problems. Maybe the problem they solve isn’t one you’ve encountered, or one that <em>stops</em> everything from working, but what if the technology just makes things <em>better</em>? Embrace that! It keeps your brain moving, and it makes you significantly more valuable to any employer.</p> <h2 id="when-to-learn">When to learn?</h2> <p>It seems this is a touchy subject for some people. Managers obviously want you to learn on your own time, but developers often seem reluctant to learn something on there time, if it’s something that is required for their job. So, when should you learn? <em>YES</em>.</p> <h3 id="learning-on-your-time">Learning on your time</h3> <p>Why are you a developer? I would assume you are a developer because you like to write code, or build interfaces, or do whatever it is you do. Why would you want to be stuck with the same old thing? It’s likely a comfort issue or something, but that’s all the more reason to learn on <em>your</em> time. Get excited about development again. Find whatever it is that lead you down this path in the first place, and find that spark again. You should love learning new things! Make time to do it! Take this time to develop your own pet project. Think of something you want to do (please, not another to-do app), and build it in a language you’ve never used. Or with a framework you’re unfamiliar with. You’ll learn something new, and possibly solve a problem for yourself.</p> <h3 id="learn-on-someone-elses-time">Learn on someone else’s time</h3> <p>Learning at work is a great thing to do! Even in the most dynamic environment, it’s still easy to become “the expert” in one particular area, or on one particular system and totally miss out on new technologies. Don’t let this happen, push to be part of new projects, or work to apply new technologies to your existing projects. I firmly believe that managers should a lot time to developers learning new things. Especially if that thing is something new to the company, or new to the project. Take time to experiment. Get training. Maybe there isn’t a budget available for training courses or conference, but set up internal training sessions with other developers. This is incredibly important if the technology is new to the company or project. For example, the company I work for first built there site in 1995. A few years later, they hired a developer who is still with us today. When she started, JavaScript wasn’t something used commonly on websites, and was certainly a lot different where it was being used. She deserves the time, at work, to advance those skills.</p> <h2 id="how-to-keep-up-to-date">How to keep up to date?</h2> <p>There are a lot of ways to keep up with changes. Depending on your situation, these may not all work, but I’m sure something will!</p> <p>First, I say <strong>get on <a href="https://twitter.com/MarkRabey">Twitter</a></strong>. Find a hashtag that fits what you’re interested in, or something you know about already. Maybe that’s <a href="https://twitter.com/search?q=%23frontenddev">#frontenddev</a>, or <a href="https://twitter.com/search?q=%23javascript">#javascript</a>. In there you’ll find blog posts, questions, tutorials, all sorts of things. Find a tutorial and <em>try it</em>. If you come across something you haven’t heard of yet, focus on that for awhile. Subscribe to newsletters and blogs. Read as much as you can, and find as many tutorials as you can. When you find these posts on Twitter, follow that user! They (along with their followers) can provide a wealth of valuable resources.</p> <p><strong>Attend conferences!</strong> This can be tough. They can be costly, but are incredibly valuable. If the content of the conference is applicable at work, ask your employer to either cover your expenses, or at the very least split them with you. Thousands of developers attend conference to share ideas, thoughts, and experiences, and to learn from others. You will hear discussions on best practices, standards, and trends. It’s a great way to meet people who do what you do.</p> <p><strong>Find Courses</strong> - Personally, I prefer to learn through tutorials, reading, experimenting, and discussions (like at conferences). But if that doesn’t work for whatever reason, try your local college. See if there is a night school course available. Ideally this would be something that helps you at work, but really, <em>anything</em> is better than learning nothing!</p> <hr /> <p>Keep your brain working, keep your skills valuable, and have fun.</p> Tue, 09 Sep 2014 00:00:00 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/09/09/keeping-up-to-date http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/09/09/keeping-up-to-date learning, full-stack, html, css, javascript, management The Full-Stack Web Developer <p>The Internet has changed a lot over the years. Not just in the way we use it, but also in the way we build it. As a result, we have new job functions being created almost daily. Just a few years ago, you needed a <strong>web designer</strong> to create the look and feel of a site, and a <strong>web developer</strong> to code the front-end and back-end scripts to make the site work. Back then, websites were charged based on the number of pages. Something I can only assume was borrowed from the print industry.</p> <p>Now, however, we have a huge list of roles, titles, and job functions:</p> <ul> <li>Web designer</li> <li>Visual Designer</li> <li>Creative Director</li> <li>Art Director</li> <li>UI Designer</li> <li>UX Designer</li> <li>Interaction Designer</li> <li>Front-end Developer</li> <li>Back-end Developer</li> <li>Web Developer</li> <li>Mobile Developer</li> <li>Database Developer</li> <li>Database Administrator</li> <li>Web Master</li> <li>Web Server Administrator</li> <li>SEO Specialist</li> <li>Web Analyst</li> <li>Quality Assurance Tester</li> <li>Project Manager</li> <li>Growth Hacker</li> <li>…</li> </ul> <p>Depending on the size of the organisation, and the number of <a href="/blog/2014/07/30/people-are-not-resources/">people</a> on team, an individual may take on multiple roles. Larger, more profitable companies tend to hire experts from each field in order to create the best products the web has to offer.</p> <p>More often, growing, or more down-to-earth companies, the old standard of distinct web designers and web developers still exists. The designer is typically responsible for the visual/web/UI/UX/interaction design, while the developer will do the front-end coding / back-end coding / database administration /server setup / debugging / etc. However, problems often arise when the two have to work together, but only one of them take responsibility for the project. This can be caused by miscommunication, misunderstanding of roles, misunderstanding of technology, or many other reasons that ultimately lead to a sub-par product.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="enter-the-full-stack-web-developer">Enter the Full-Stack Web Developer</h2> <p>Having a full-stack web developer on your team can fill some of the enormous gaps that I mentioned above. Full-stack web developers master the best of both worlds. Well, they at least <em>understand</em> both world. That’s often what you need to keep projects moving in the right direction. They can keep things on track, while implementing best practices and new technologies. They will design <em>and</em> code entirely on their own, or then will supervise others who perform the tasks, while the full-stack developer oversees the whole process from start to finish.</p> <p>As websites evolve into web application, and they become more mature and interactive, it’s critical to have an understanding of the technical capacity of a system, and having an eye for aesthetic and usability. It’s almost natural for web professionals to evolve into full-stack developers.</p> <p>All that said, it’s important to know that designing and coding are to very different skills that require different parts of the brain to master. The right brain is used for visual and creativity while the left brain is used for structure and organisation. For this reason designers are primarily right-brained, and coders are left-brained. With that thought in mind, I do believe that web designers have to use a good chunk of the left brain as well in order to fit pieces of a page together. That involves a huge amount of structure and organisation. Furthermore, coders will use their right brain to come up with <em>creative</em> ways of solving things. After a conversation with a co-worker, I feel like I need to clarify I’m not saying that one side of the brain is used <em>exclusively</em>, rather <em>predominantly</em>.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="why-be-a-full-stack-web-developer">Why Be a Full-Stack Web Developer?</h2> <p>As I learn more about web development, I truly think of myself as a full-stack web developer. While I have certainly not <em>mastered</em> design or coding, I have a pretty solid understanding of both. I have developed project management skills, I have the skills to communicate with business people, designers, Linux users, and junior programmers. I can teach people. I can solve problems. Being able to do what I do makes me more valuable to an employer. I can be a member of cross-functional Agile teams, and perform either front-end or back-end development. I can work with business analysts and managers to design a product. And most important (to me, today) I can start my own web design and development business. (Coming soon).</p> <p><img src="/content/images/blog/2014/Aug/full-stack.jpg" alt="Full-Stack" /></p> Fri, 15 Aug 2014 14:42:00 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/08/15/the-full-stack-web-developer http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/08/15/the-full-stack-web-developer full-stack, front-end, back-end, html, css People Are Not Resources <p>While I like the fact that some changes are starting to happen with our processes at work, I also find my self rather disappointed in some of the changes. They’re not happening as fast as I’d like, but that’s something I just need to come to terms with. Some of the changes that I was told were going to happen are now seeming like a pipe-dream. That’s honestly a little hurtful. But the thing that’s bothering me the most, is some of the new ‘lingo’ I’m hearing in meetings and in general conversation around the office.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="what-is-a-resource">What is a ‘resource’?</h2> <p>I’ve heard it in the office before, but very rarely. Lately, however, it has become common among a few co-workers (mainly managers) to refer to the people who ‘do’ things (in our case it’s web developers) as ‘resources’.</p> <blockquote> <p>“I need to schedule my resources.”<br /></p> </blockquote> <blockquote> <p>“We don’t have enough resources, we need more bodies.”<br /></p> </blockquote> <blockquote> <p>“Which resources are working on that?”</p> </blockquote> <p>I’m sad to say that this terminology is becoming the norm. This is incredibly offensive, to say the least. It implies that despite being educated, experienced, and passionate, we are:</p> <ul> <li>on the same level as ‘materials’</li> <li>as disposable as a paper clip</li> <li>identical in skills, knowledge, and abilities</li> <li>boundlessly interchangeable and replaceable</li> <li>little more than numbers on a balance sheet</li> </ul> <p>Clearly, this is inaccurate.</p> <p>There is a lot written about the sociological structure of development teams, and that’s far beyond the scope of this post, however it should be apparent to anyone who has worked in a development environment, that the above statements are 100% false. So, why is this so common?</p> <p>When thinking about why managers use this word, the best reason I can think of is that it makes it easier to change the lives of their coworkers. I mean, it’s much nicer to say you’re “reallocating a resource” rather than, moving someone off a project. It’s hard to fault someone for that. I think it comes from an approach to simplify the development process. It’s a broken approach that basically has people trying to think of development like a factory production line. Analysts and Project Managers stuff requirements in one end, developers are in the middle making the ‘goods’, which are then sent to ‘testers’, or ‘test resources’ who allow a production quality product to spit out the other end.</p> <p>This type of environment means your developers are simply ‘resources’; universal units of man-power. It suggests that the more ‘resources’ you assign to a project, the faster the work will be completed. This may be very true in a factory. If three people (or machines?) packaging frozen pizzas can package 3000 pizzas per hours, it should be reasonable to expect that six people could package 6000 pizzas per hour, right? Probably. So, the same is true of software development, right? Two ‘resources’ can finish it in two weeks, so let’s just stick two more ‘resources’ on it and get it finished in one week. Problem solved! Not exactly.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="not-the-same">Not the same</h2> <p>It might just be something we face where I work, though I doubt it, but none of our developers are the same. Obviously, everyone has different personalities and things, but I mean our skills and abilities are different. What might take one person 2 days might take another person 4 hours. That’s probably part of a different issue, but either way, we are not infinitely movable from project to project, we are not equally valuable, and we are equally skilled. We each offer very different things to a project. ___ <img src="http://dilbert.com/dyn/str_strip/000000000/00000000/0000000/000000/20000/1000/100/21111/21111.strip.gif" alt="People, not resources." /></p> <hr /> <h2 id="pure-poison">Pure Poison</h2> <p>The word ‘resource’ is poisonous in a development environment. Using it only enlarges the gap between shop floor drones (you know, the highly skilled developers) and the ‘management’. Behind closed doors, managers shuffle ‘resources’ and try to make some sense of the scheduling mess they created with their improper outlook on projects. To me, it implies a lack of understanding of software development. It also grossly devalues, and discourages your best performers. Suggesting that all ‘resources’ are the same means that workers need to somehow be <em>equalized</em>. In reality, this requires pulling the top down, while dragging the bottom up.</p> <p>Really, it’s pretty simple. I am not a stapler, or a paper clip. I am not a shop floor drone, or some sort of machine. I have unique skills and abilities to offer, and as such, I am not interchangeable with my team members. People are not resources.</p> Wed, 30 Jul 2014 11:28:26 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/07/30/people-are-not-resources http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/07/30/people-are-not-resources management, people, scrum, agile, team "Good Enough", Isn't <p>It’s something that plagues many businesses, both small and large. Often business owners get into a particular mind-set when it comes to design projects. They assume that that can, and should, handle everything in-house rather than pay someone else to do it. First of all, there is no good reason why anyone <em>should</em> do everything. However, more importantly, it’s ridiculous to think that you <em>can</em> do everything.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="does-size-matter">Does Size Matter?</h2> <p>The short answer (no pun intended) is “no”. Regardless of how big or small your business is, you should consider hiring a designer. Maybe you hire that person to be the in-house resource you need, or maybe you hire that person for just this one, inexpensive (<a href="/blog/2014/05/29/should-designers-and-developers-work-for-free/">not free</a>) job. Regardless of the term you hire someone for, it’s important to have someone dedicated to <em>that</em> job, with knowledge of <em>that</em> job. I’m sorry, but having someone who’s “pretty good with that stuff” is not the same as having someone who can actually do the job <em>well</em>. A large company needs someone so they can maintain the brand and reputation of the company, while a small business needs to <em>develop</em> and <em>maintain</em> that brand.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="why-do-you-have-designs">Why Do You Have Designs?</h2> <p>What is it that you are designing? What is the project? Yes, everything is a project. Regardless of size, time line, or complexity, if you are producing something, it’s a project. What are these projects?</p> <p>When is comes to design, these projects can be anything really. Web designers build sites and applications, for both internal and external users, while graphic designers often build advertising and branding materials. The main point, regardless of the final product, is that you are creating something, for someone. What that person feels when they look at that end product has a direct, and profound impact on what they think of your business.</p> <p>As I mentioned in a <a href="/blog/2014/05/29/should-designers-and-developers-work-for-free/">previous post</a>, the reason you’re designing <em>something</em> is most likely for some sort of advertising, marketing, or branding. It’s worth investing in well made, and well <em>designed</em>, marketing materials that either maintain or develop your brand. Do you really want your logo, advertisement, or web site to be just “OK”, or worse?</p> <p>I’m sorry to say it, but “Good Enough”, isn’t. <img src="/content/images/blog/2014/Jul/good-enough-24400-300x214.jpeg" alt="Good Enough" /></p> Fri, 11 Jul 2014 08:26:44 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/07/11/good-enough-isnt http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/07/11/good-enough-isnt management, learning, freelance Lean Software Development <p>Back in May, I wrote a post called <em><a href="/blog/2014/05/07/the-process-and-the-team/">The Process and the Team</a></em>. It was kind of an extension of an even earlier post, <em><a href="/blog/2014/04/21/recognizing-team-dysfunctions/">Recognizing Team Dysfunctions</a></em>. In that I mentioned a few methodologies we could possibly use instead of Scrum, which I still firmly believe won’t work for our currently team. In writing that post, I read a lot about Lean Software Development. Now, a few months later, with much consideration, I still think it’s a great option to consider.</p> <p>As always, my ideas are based on the “team” I currently work on, so the issues I’m faced with may be very different from those of others. As well, I am not trained in Agile Development (Scrum, Lean, or any other method). I’m just a guy with both ideas and frustrations. So, without all the other ‘stuff’ from those other posts, here is Lean Software Development, in a nutshell…I think.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="lean-software-development">Lean Software Development</h2> <p>Derived from the manufacturing method of the same name, Lean Software Development (LSD) is fairly new method, that is gaining a lot of acceptance in the Agile community. It is based on 7 principles:</p> <ol> <li><strong>Eliminate waste</strong> <ul> <li>Anything that does not add value to the customer is regarded as waste. In order to eliminate waste, it must be properly recognized. If an activity can be bypassed, or the result achieved more efficiently, it is waste. Incomplete, or unused features are waste. Often things that don’t add immediate “value” to the project are seen as waste, but are actually quite valuable in the long run. These things include adequate testing, code reviews, and clear requirements.</li> </ul> </li> <li><strong>Amplify learning</strong> <ul> <li>Development is a constant learning process. Rather than over complicated planning, new ideas can be tried my writing code and building. Allow teams to create ‘proof-of-concept’ projects in order to gain more knowledge. Recognize that this code will likely not ever make it to a production environment, but some version of it may be re-factored into the final product. This adds value, by adding knowledge.</li> </ul> </li> <li><strong>Decide as late as possible</strong> <ul> <li>Delaying decisions as much as possible allows them to be based on facts, not assumptions or predictions. All systems should be built with a great capacity for change. Planning should be concentrated on different options, and clarify confusing situations while establishing processes for rapid action.</li> </ul> </li> <li><strong>Deliver as fast as possible</strong> <ul> <li>By delivering small iterations at a fast pace, a team is able to receive and act on feedback sooner. Smaller iterations of a product results in better learning and communication within the team. Delivering quickly, means decisions can be delayed. This means ensures that the team produces the product that fits the current need, not what was required yesterday. This means customers can delay decisions until such time they know what it is they truly require.</li> </ul> </li> <li><strong>Empower the team</strong> <ul> <li>Rather than telling developers how to do their jobs, managers are taught to listen to developers explain what actions they might take, as well as provide suggestions for improvement. Allowing developers to find their own solutions can encourage progress, catch more errors, and remove impediments, without micro-managing.</li> </ul> </li> <li><strong>Build integrity in</strong> <ul> <li>Conceptual integrity means the the systems components all work well together. The system is flexible, efficient, and maintainable. This can potentially be achieved by gaining an understanding of a problem while solving it at the same time. The information flow should be constant and occur directly between developers and customers or managers. One way to toward this integrity is constant refactoring of code. This keeps the code maintainable as new features are added, and forces developers to revisit solutions they have come up with in the past.</li> </ul> </li> <li><strong>See the whole</strong> <ul> <li>Websites and software systems are not just a group of functions, and piles of code. Rather, they are the result of the interaction of those functions. Problems with software tend to increase during development. By breaking big tasks into smaller, more manageable pieces, and by standardizing the development process, the root cause of these defects could be found and removed fairly quickly.</li> </ul> </li> </ol> <blockquote> <p>Think big, act small, fail fast; learn rapidly.<br /> - <em><strong>Lean Software Development</strong> by Mary and Tom Poppendieck (2003).</em></p> </blockquote> <p>The practices (or “tools” as the Poppendiecks call them) are a little different from those in many of Agile methods, but there are obvious similarities:</p> <ul> <li>Seeing waste <ul> <li>learning what is valuable to the customer is not an easy task. It doesn’t just mean dollars.</li> </ul> </li> <li>Value stream mapping <ul> <li>analyzing current work flows, and create new or refined flows</li> </ul> </li> <li>Pull systems <ul> <li>As in Kanban, a pull system creates a sort of “working backlog” with a set maximum amount of work in progress</li> </ul> </li> <li>Queuing theory <ul> <li>by studying wait times over time, predictions can be made on when work might be completed</li> </ul> </li> <li>Motivation <ul> <li>motivate team members to learn more, and produce better products</li> </ul> </li> <li>Measurements <ul> <li>find and set measurable goals for the team</li> <li>this can guide the above motivation ___ Using <em>Lean</em>, would give developers a sense of ownership and control over projects, while allowing managers to set reasonable deadlines. It would also increase the visibility of projects. Developers would be more a part of the process than they are when using other methods, and have more direct access to stakeholders.</li> </ul> </li> </ul> Tue, 08 Jul 2014 09:48:08 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/07/08/lean-software-development http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/07/08/lean-software-development management, people, scrum, agile, team Meta Descriptions <p>A meta description is an HTML attribute that gives a brief explanation of the contents of a web page. These are often used on search engine results pages to give a preview of the given page.</p> <p><strong>Code Sample:</strong></p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="nt">&lt;head&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;meta</span> <span class="na">name=</span><span class="s">&quot;description&quot;</span> <span class="na">content=</span><span class="s">&quot;This is a sample of a description. This is what will, most likely, appear in search results.&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/head&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <hr /> <h2 id="whats-a-meta-description">What’s a Meta Description?</h2> <p>Although not important to search engine <em>ranking</em>, meta descriptions are incredibly important to click-throughs from search engine results pages. These brief summaries of your page are your opportunity to advertise content to search engine users, and let them know exactly whether or not a given page contains the information they are searching for.</p> <p>The meta descriptions should make intelligent use of keywords, while also creating a coercive description that makes a user <em>want</em> to click. A good meta description is both relevant and unique to the page. Ideally this description is between 150-160 characters.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="best-practices">Best Practices</h2> <h2 id="compelling-copy">Compelling Copy</h2> <p>The content of your meta description is essentially your advertising copy. In most cases, this is what users see on search engine result pages, and therefore are an exceptionally important part of your marketing. Creating a coherent, interesting description, that contains important keywords, can drastically increase the click-through rate of a webpage.</p> <h2 id="recommended-length">Recommended Length</h2> <p>Meta descriptions <em>can</em> be any length, although search engines typically truncate descriptions long that 160 characters. As well, if a description is too short, a search engine may display other content from the site in hopes of giving users a more accurate snippet on results pages. In fact, at the time of writing, that’s the case on my homepage. My current description is “I do web stuff”. This is not nearly long enough. More at that shortly.</p> <h2 id="avoid-duplicates">Avoid Duplicates</h2> <p>Just like <a href="/blog/2014/07/02/title-tags/">title tags</a>, it’s important to have each meta description be unique. <img src="/content/images/blog/2014/Jul/Screen-Shot-2014-07-08-at-4-11-20-PM.png" alt="Truncated Titles and Duplicate Descriptions" /> You can see in the above image, that the title are truncated, and the descriptions are identical. If these were posts on my website, the user would have no clear way of understanding, at a glance, what the content of the page might be. A nice way to make this work is to generate dynamic or programmatic descriptions for automated pages. For example, on my site, the meta description for each post, is the same excerpt that I use on my homepage. I know that it is the appropriate length, and unless I’ve duplicated my own posts, it’s also unique.</p> <h2 id="not-a-google-ranking-factor">Not a Google Ranking Factor</h2> <p>It’s true! In fact, Google doesn’t use keywords as a ranking factor either. They do however, use these meta tags for other things. Again, this is what they usually use as the preview snippet search results pages. They also use meta descriptions to gather results when a user uses advanced search functions to match meta tag content.</p> <h2 id="quotes-kill-descriptions">Quotes Kill Descriptions</h2> <p>As soon as Google finds a quote in a meta description, it gets cut off. It’s just the way their crawlers function. To avoid this issue, it’s best to remove any non-alphanumeric characters from your meta description. If you <em>must</em> have quotes, using single quotes is a safe way to prevent truncation.</p> <h2 id="sometimes-you-can-skip-it">Sometimes You Can Skip It</h2> <p>I honestly don’t know that I agree here, but I read it somewhere, and the thought intrigued me. The idea is, if your page only focuses on 1-3 keywords, it’s easy to fit them into a meta description. However, if your site potentially has hundreds of articles or blog post, or a large product listing, than it might be a better idea to let the search engines extract the content themselves. When a search engine pulls content, they always display the keywords (and neighbouring text) that the user has searched for. If you force a meta description, that could actually draw away from the choice of text the search engine would have made on its own. For this reason, I might actually be better off <em>not</em> adding a longer meta description on my homepage.</p> <p>Whether you choose to write a meta description or not, another thing to consider is social media. Facebook, for example, commonly uses a page’s meta description when the page is posted on their site. Without this, social media sites may just use the <em>first text on your page</em>, and that might create a negative user experience. For this reason, I think I actually like my “too short” meta description. It means Google will display the <em>most</em> relevant content, based on the search term, while sites like Facebook will show my meta description: “<em>I do web stuff</em>”.</p> Wed, 02 Jul 2014 14:11:03 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/07/02/meta-descriptions http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/07/02/meta-descriptions html, seo, meta tags Keep it simple <p>I started out by want to create a <a href="/blog/2014/04/07/a-simple-css-grid/">simple CSS grid</a>. Eventually I decided to turn that into a <a href="/blog/2014/05/20/css-frameworks/">CSS framework</a>. Then I wrote about <a href="/blog/2014/05/26/making-the-grid-responsive/">making the grid responsive</a>. While these posts are all very relevant, I have decided that really, in the end all I want is a grid.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="what-is-a-framework">What is a framework?</h2> <blockquote> <p><strong><em>frame·work</em></strong><br /> /ˈfrāmˌwərk/</p> </blockquote> <blockquote> <p><em>noun</em><br /></p> </blockquote> <blockquote> <pre><code>* an essential supporting structure of a building, vehicle, or object. * a basic structure underlying a system, concept, or text. </code></pre> </blockquote> <p>I probably should have looked at this definition before attempting to build a framework. It would have made me think about what is actually <em>essential</em> to every site.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="what-is-essential">What is <em>essential</em>?</h2> <p>Upon reflection, I’ve decided that I’ve entirely over-complicated my framework. I came to this conclusion when I was building what I hoped would serve as the demo page for the framework. I wanted a certain font, so I added that to the Framework. I wanted a navigation bar, so I added a <code>.navbar</code> component, with background colours and everything! The page was looking great, but luckily I realized that if I continued on this path, I was in <strong>big</strong> trouble. The whole reason I want to create my own framework in the first place is because I was tired of needing to override so much stuff in Bootstrap. So, on my next project, instead of overriding Bootstrap, I’d be overriding my own Framework. What does that accomplish? Every project is going to be different in terms of navigation, fonts, colours, etc. But to me, there are still a few things I want in <em>every</em> project I do. Again, these are what <em>I</em> think are essential, <em>to me</em>.</p> <ul> <li>Some simple JavaScript <ul> <li>I don’t use jQuery unless I <em>really</em> need to. Which is happen less and less. I want a simple JavaScript library with some functions that I use in just about <em>every</em> project.</li> </ul> </li> <li>Grid <ul> <li>not every site works off a grid, and that’s ok, but I like a grid, so my framework <em>must</em> have a grid. And it must be responsive.</li> </ul> </li> <li>Normalize.js <ul> <li>this helps make older browsers render similarly to modern browsers.</li> </ul> </li> <li>Mixins <ul> <li>A bunch of ‘functions’ in my LESS files that make writing things like transitions, gradients, and drop-shadows much easier.</li> </ul> </li> <li>Base Styles <ul> <li>a few <em>very basic</em> styles - slight margin on tags like <code>table</code>, <code>ul</code>, and <code>blockquote</code>.</li> </ul> </li> <li>Easily overridden variables I can use throughout a project <ul> <li>grid cell counts</li> <li>font sizes (headers 1-6)</li> <li>font colours</li> <li>gray scale</li> </ul> </li> </ul> <p>A note on the last one - an emphasis on the ‘easily overridden’ part. This really just acts as a place to hold all these types of variables in any project.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="what-do-you-get">What do you get?</h2> <p>Using this Framework, I get just the basics. I only get what I use in <em>every</em> project. I don’t have icons, fonts, typography, navigation, buttons, drop downs, or anything like that. The fact is, I don’t use most of these things. Certainly not in <em>every</em> project. What I <em>do</em> get is a lightweight base for every project. I get a framework. I call it <a href="https://github.com/MarkRabey/GroundWork&quot;target=&quot;_blank">GroundWork</a>.</p> <hr /> <p>You can find <a href="https://github.com/MarkRabey/GroundWork&quot;target=&quot;_blank">GroundWork</a> on <a href="https://github.com/MarkRabey/GroundWork&quot;target=&quot;_blank">GitHub</a>.</p> Thu, 12 Jun 2014 09:22:56 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/06/12/keep-it-simple http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/06/12/keep-it-simple css, framework, layout, simple Quote Tweets with a Node.js Twitter Bot <p>A short time ago I wrote about <a href="/blog/2014/05/09/build-a-twitter-bot-with-node-js/">how to build a Twitter bot with Node.js</a>. Making use of the Twitter API, I have a bot that searches for Tweets with either #CFML or #ColdFusion, then retweets the most recent one. Pretty simple, and maybe not even 100% practical, but it’s all I was looking to do. A reader asked me if it was possible to Quote Tweet rather than Retweet.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="whats-the-difference">What’s the difference?</h2> <p>Nothing really. They’re basically just 2 different ways to accomplish the same thing: share someone else’s Tweet with your followers. The technical difference is, when retweeting, the original Tweet is left unmodified, with the same ID number, and simply shared with your followers, while quote Tweets are <em>copying</em> the text of a Tweet into a <em>new</em> Tweet that your followers see as being from <em>you</em>. Quote Tweets are a nice way to add your own comment to a Tweet, but can have issues since you’re still limited to 140 characters.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="how-do-we-do-it-with-the-api">How do we do it with the API?</h2> <p>Strictly speaking, you can’t. There is not a direct API method for quote Tweets. You can, however, just build your own body to update your own status. From the original <a href="/blog/2014/05/09/build-a-twitter-bot-with-node-js/">bot I wrote</a>, the code looked like this:</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-javascript" data-lang="javascript"><span class="kd">var</span> <span class="nx">Twit</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="nx">require</span><span class="p">(</span><span Over the years I have seen a very disturbing trend. We are often asked to do work for <strong>free</strong>.</p> <p>Friends, family, even clients or potential clients, often ask us to work for free, or a rock bottom price.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="why-should-i-work-for-free">Why <em>should</em> I work for free?</h2> <blockquote> <p>“You can use it to build your portfolio.”</p> </blockquote> <blockquote> <p>“If this goes well, there’s lots more work for you!”</p> </blockquote> <blockquote> <p>“It will make us all money in the long term.”</p> </blockquote> <p>These are just a few of the things I’ve heard over the years. Don’t get me wrong, I understand everyone wants a deal, and yes, generally I’m willing to negotiate a price. So, in response to your suggestions above:</p> <ul> <li>I don’t need your project to boost my portfolio.</li> <li>Pay me for the work I do. If it goes well, I’ll accept more work from you.</li> <li>If you’re not willing to invest in the project now, I doubt that it will ever really get off the ground and make anyone money.</li> </ul> <hr /> <h2 id="how-would-you-like-your-design">How would you like your design?</h2> <p>The old triad for design was “fast, cheap, good: pick 2”. You can add “free” as one of the options, but the results likely aren’t what anyone really wants. <img src="/content/images/blog/2014/May/graphic-design-infographic-01.jpg" alt="How would you like your design?" /></p> <hr /> <h2 id="why-must-i-be-paid">Why <em>must</em> I be paid?</h2> <p>There are several reasons we need to be paid. In addition to investing in quality work, it’s important to remember these important points about your designer/developer:</p> <ol> <li><strong>This is our job</strong>. That’s not to say we don’t love what we do, but we do it to make a living and support our families. Even if it’s not our full-time job, it’s what we’ve chosen as our career. We have spent a lot of time and money to do what we do. And we do it well.</li> <li><strong>You pay your plumber</strong>. Would you even dream of asking a plumber to fix your pipes for free? Would you ask him to cut his price to give you “a break”. Just because we love what we do, doesn’t mean it’s not valuable.</li> <li><strong>The Phrase “Quick Tweak” is a lie</strong>. Most of these are neither ‘quick’ nor ‘tweaks’. Remember, you hired us because we’re the ones that know what <em>is</em> quick, and what <em>is</em> a tweak. But when you say “this shouldn’t be too hard, just bang it out”, we actually feel physically ill. Really, for us to be happy, only one kind of work will satisfy us: <strong><em>Great</em></strong>.</li> <li><strong>We are not heartless</strong>. We are usually willing to work on prices, and payment schedules with you. Particularly for large projects. We <em>do</em> free work for schools, charities, and non-profits, but <em>we</em> get to choose who and when.</li> </ol> <hr /> <h2 id="bottom-line">Bottom Line</h2> <p>Chances are, if you need a graphic designer you’re promoting something. It might be a business (logos, brochures, cards, signs, etc.) or maybe an event of some sort. If you’re hiring a web designer or developer, you’re likely working on creating or updating a web site. In either case, the purpose of these is most likely to generate <em>revenue</em>. Work on a realistic budget for <em>great</em> design. Do you really want your logo, newspaper ad, or web site to be just “OK”, or worse?</p> Thu, 29 May 2014 10:08:45 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/05/29/should-designers-and-developers-work-for-free http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/05/29/should-designers-and-developers-work-for-free CSS Frameworks <p>I’ve done it again. Sort of. I’ve worked with something long enough to develop it into a great proof of concept, then more or less abandon it. This time it was my <a href="/blog/2014/04/07/a-simple-css-grid/">CSS Grid</a>. I haven’t totally given up on it, I will still use it and develop it over time, but I’m putting it on the back burner. What I initially planned on build was <em>just</em> a grid. But there is a lot more to a page than the grid.</p> <p>It turns out that what I <strong><em>actually</em></strong> want to build is my own framework. Something I can work with on multiple projects, with class names that I like, written in a way I like them. This is still something I intend on doing, and this grid I’ve written <em>will</em> be used in it, but right now, I don’t have the time to commit to a project of this scale. I need my own theme for my blog (something I tried to do, but was unhappy with the result), as well, I have work to do on a few projects for clients.</p> <p>So, I’m going to build templates and samples using <a href="http://getbootstrap.com&quot;target=&quot;_blank">Twitter Bootstrap</a> for now, as I am very comfortable with it.</p> <h2 id="is-a-framework-ok">Is a Framework Ok?</h2> <p>In short: <strong>YES</strong>. Frameworks are great. Frameworks make a lot of things easy. Rather than write the same code over and over for each project, a framework gives you a foundation to build from. It does <strong><em>not</em></strong> make you less of a designer or developer to reuse code.</p> <p>That said, there are a few things to keep in mind when using a framework. These are the things that make me want to write one strictly for me.</p> <h2 id="styles">Styles</h2> <p>Popular CSS Frameworks impose a lot of styles on the developer. This can be ok, especially if you’re not the best when it comes to design. The look and feel of the frameworks elements might be exactly what you want or need. However, you might spend a lot of time overwriting styles from the framework.</p> <h2 id="bloat">Bloat</h2> <p>How much of the framework are you actually going to be using? Depending on how you customize your download, you may and up include hundreds or even thousands of lines of CSS that never get used in your project.</p> <p>The same can be said for plugins. Bootstrap, for example, requires you to include <a href="http://jquery.com&quot;target=&quot;_blank">jQuery</a> in order to use dropdowns, tooltips, modals, etc. This is perfectly understandable, but again, if you’re not making use of any or all of these, do you need to include jQuery?</p> <hr /> <p>The list of Pros and Cons could keep going for me. Really, it comes down to what you’re looking to accomplish. Right now, for me, if I need to develop something quickly, and easily, I’m using a framework. However, I will be working on developing my own framework to define my styles.</p> Fri, 16 May 2014 20:25:47 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/05/16/css-frameworks http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/05/16/css-frameworks css, html, grid layout, frameworks Measuring Developer Productivity <p>As I’ve mentioned before, I am <strong><em>not</em></strong> an expert in this area, but I am really passionate about teams working well together and producing high quality code. And I think I have some pretty good ideas. Although I am not a manager, it’s easy to see that managing a team of developers is <em>really</em> hard. Not only because of developers having split personalities, but also in evaluating their productivity.</p> <h2 id="why-evaluate-developers">Why Evaluate Developers?</h2> <p>I think it’s safe to say, that just about everybody wants validation in their lives. It’s important to know that what you’re doing is not only appreciated, but also valued. Sometimes verbal recognition is sufficient. Giving a “pat on the back”, especially in front of other team members, can often be a major pick up for a developer. But that doesn’t work long term. Things will come up. Raises, bonuses, and promotions, are all things that will likely need to be discussed at some point. It’s important to be able to measure an individual’s productivity when evaluating these things, and providing feedback, especially if they need to <em>improve</em> on something.</p> <h2 id="whats-so-hard-about-that">What’s So Hard About That?</h2> <p>It’s fairly easy to evaluate something like sales for example. You can clearly see how much revenue a salesperson is generating at any given point. From that you can set first-quarter goals, second-quarter goals, etc. Also, there are a number of great tools for measuring the productivity of <strong><em>teams</em></strong> and <strong><em>projects</em></strong>, however, with <strong><em>individuals</em></strong>, there is a huge number of variables when it comes to measuring productivity. The number of lines of code? The speed you type at? The business value of the project you <em>happen</em> to be assigned to? Application development is an incredibly complex process. It involves both detailed analysis, and on-the-fly creativity. It sometimes even involves starring at your monitor for a few hours. This makes it nearly impossible to measure objectively.</p> <h2 id="what-can-we-do">What <em>Can</em> We Do?</h2> <p>This is where managers are left with a major challenge. They somehow need to be fair, while evaluating and measuring <strong><em>subjectively</em></strong>. <em>Can you do that?</em></p> <p>There really aren’t any objective measurements, so measuring subjectively is all you have left. I guess you could continue to <em>not</em> measure, but I think that leads to unguided team members. That’s not fun. I believe managers do this subjective evaluating anyway. Again, I’m not a manager (yet), but I know <em>I</em> would do that. Maybe not totally on purpose, but it’s safe to say managers are able to look around the room and know who the top producing coders are. Who would you ask first to fix a critical bug in the system? If it needs to go out <em>yesterday</em>, who’s best to jump on it? You know who is more valuable to the company. You’ve evaluated it, or “you just know”. So, what are you considering?</p> <p>I would suggest managers actually write down the things they think make those “top coders” their “top coders”. Have a “formal” subjective evaluation process. Essentially a checklist for evaluating productivity. Some of these involve have standards set by the team, but that’s for another post.</p> <p>Assuming those standards are in place, here’s what I have so far:</p> <ul> <li><strong>Productivity</strong></li> <li>do they produce code at a reasonable pace?</li> <li>is their velocity on bug fixing sufficient?</li> <li><strong>Engagement</strong></li> <li>are they dedicated to their role?</li> <li>are they committed to delivering on time?</li> <li>are the committed to the success of the team/company?</li> <li><strong>Attention to Quality</strong></li> <li>does their code work as designed?</li> <li>do they adequately test their code before calling it ‘done’?</li> <li>are there minimal bugs reported against their code?</li> <li>do they write unit tests for all their new code?</li> <li>do they follow the <a href="http://programmer.97things.oreilly.com/wiki/index.php/The_Boy_Scout_Rule&quot;target=&quot;_blank">Boy Scout Rule</a> and leave code cleaner than they found it?</li> <li><strong>System Knowledge and Usage</strong></li> <li>how well do they understand the code base they are working with?</li> <li>do they take responsibility for the team’s code base, always trying to improve it?</li> <li><strong>Adherence to Coding Guidelines and Techniques</strong></li> <li>does the developers code meet coding standards?</li> <li>do code reviews reveal minimal issues with their code?</li> <li><strong>Learning and Skills</strong></li> <li>are they constantly learning and improving their skills?</li> <li>do they show a passion for software/application/web development?</li> <li><strong>Personal Responsibility</strong></li> <li>do they first assume the error is in <em>their</em> code?</li> <li>do they understand that they are solely responsible for their code working?</li> <li>do they take pride in their code, making sure it’s clean, easy to read, and maintainable?</li> </ul> <p>Again, these are very subjective measurements. They require the manager to make very subjective judgments. But I would think that they are in a management position because they have good judgment about these things. And, let’s face it, they’re probably doing this already.</p> <p>By simply writing these things down and discussing them openly with individual developers, you can not only evaluate productivity, but also provide feedback. Most of these questions could have “yes” or “no” answers, but they can all lead to further discussion. These discussions can help less productive developers know where they need to improve, and help more productive developers understand where they might be headed within the company.</p> <p>While subjective evaluations may not be <em>ideal</em>, they are far better than <em>no</em> evaluations.</p> Wed, 14 May 2014 14:34:10 -0400 http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/05/14/measuring-developer-productivity http://markrabey.com/blog/2014/05/14/measuring-developer-productivity Semantic HTML <p>This is a really hot topic among web geeks. Often the group is pretty split. I think everyone <em>agrees</em> that semantics are a good thing, but the split comes in understanding what that means. When is comes to HTML, semantics basically means describing the content of a tag, without specifying it. ___</p> <h2 id="bad-semantics">Bad Semantics</h2> <p>Bad semantics means using tags that don’t describe their content. I’ve actually seen production code that looked something like this:</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="nt">&lt;div</span> <span class="na">class=</span><span class="s">&quot;article&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;div</span> <span class="na">class=</span><span class="s">&quot;article_header&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span>Article Title<span class="nt">&lt;/div&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;div</span> <span class="na">class=</span><span class="s">&quot;article_content&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span> This is the content of the article. I want some <span class="nt">&lt;div</span> <span class="na">class=</span><span class="s">&quot;bold_text&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span>bold text<span class="nt">&lt;/div&gt;</span>. <span class="nt">&lt;/div&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/div&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <p>The issue here is that <code>&lt;div&gt;</code> in no way describes what is in side. Then a very specific class is added in order to style as needed.</p> <hr /> <h2 id="good-semantics">Good Semantics</h2> <p>While the above code may have been written prior to HTML5, older versions of HTML also had semantic tags, such as <code>&lt;form&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;table&gt;</code>, or <code>&lt;img&gt;</code>. The content of these tags is clearly defined. In, say, HTML4, the above code probably should have been written something like this:</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="nt">&lt;div</span> <span class="na">class=</span><span class="s">&quot;article&quot;</span><span class="nt">&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;h1&gt;</span>Article Title<span class="nt">&lt;/h1&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;p&gt;</span> This is the content of the article. I want some <span class="nt">&lt;b&gt;</span>bold text<span class="nt">&lt;/b&gt;</span>. <span class="nt">&lt;/p&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/div&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <p>This is better, but still not great. The tags <code>&lt;div&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;span&gt;</code> still don’t describe their content. So we are left with <code>&lt;div class="article"&gt;</code> to try to describe the content of the <code>&lt;div&gt;</code>. HTML5 introduced several new tags to make things even more semantic. Now this code could be written like this:</p> <div class="highlight"><pre><code class="language-html" data-lang="html"><span class="nt">&lt;article&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;header&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;h1&gt;</span>Article Title<span class="nt">&lt;/h1&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/header&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;p&gt;</span> This is the content of the article. I want some <span class="nt">&lt;strong&gt;</span>bold text<span class="nt">&lt;/strong&gt;</span>. <span class="nt">&lt;/p&gt;</span> <span class="nt">&lt;/article&gt;</span></code></pre></div> <p>Each tag <em>describes</em> its content, but does not <em>specify</em> the content. The <code>&lt;article&gt;</code> tag contains the article, the <code>&lt;header&gt;</code> tag, the header, the <code>&lt;p&gt;</code> tag a paragraph. The <code>&lt;b&gt;</code> tag gets replaced with the <code>&lt;strong&gt;</code> tag. In HTML 4.01, <code>&lt;strong&gt;</code> was used to define emphasized text, while in HTML5 it is used to define important text. The way to think about this from a semantic standpoint, is that we want this text to stand out, and be “strong”, but that may not necessarily mean ‘bold’. ___</p> <h2 id="staying-semantic">Staying Semantic</h2> <p>It’s tough to remain semantic sometimes. Especially in world where we’re still supporting a <strong>huge</strong> amount of old code. It can be tough to remember all the new elements available in HTML5, but it can be really valuable. For myself, I try to follow a couple really simple guidelines.</p> <p>First, I don’t use any tags just because they are styled a certain way. HTML is for my layout, CSS is for styling. Period. In fact, although still supported by most browsers, many of these tags have been deprecated in HTML5. Tags like <code>&lt;font&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;center&gt;</code>, and <code>&lt;big&gt;</code> are no longer supported. As well, many formerly common attributes, have been removed from tags. For example, the tags <code>&lt;body&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;table&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;thead&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;tbody&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;tfoot&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;tr&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;td&gt;</code>, and <code>&lt;th&gt;</code> no longer have a <code>background</code> attribute, but should instead be styled using CSS. For a complete list of obsolete tags and attributes, read <a href="http://www.w3.org/TR/html5/obsolete.html&quot;target=&quot;_blank">this document</a>. Most of these are style related.</p> <p>The only other thing I really do, is try to use the <em>best</em> tag I can. I actually try to avoid <code>&lt;div&gt;</code> and <code>&lt;span&gt;</code> tags altogether. Sometimes this doesn’t work, and I end up using them, <code>&lt;span&gt;</code> probably more than <code>&lt;div&gt;</code>, but in most cases I can use another element.
